reference PyTorch source (the prompt the agent sees)
import torch
import torch.nn as nn

class Model(nn.Module):
    """
    Model that performs a convolution, adds a bias term, scales, applies sigmoid, and performs group normalization.
    """
    def __init__(self, in_channels, out_channels, kernel_size, num_groups, bias_shape, scale_shape):
        super(Model, self).__init__()
        self.conv = nn.Conv2d(in_channels, out_channels, kernel_size)
        self.bias = nn.Parameter(torch.randn(bias_shape)) 
        self.scale = nn.Parameter(torch.randn(scale_shape))
        self.group_norm = nn.GroupNorm(num_groups, out_channels)

    def forward(self, x):
        x = self.conv(x)
        x = x + self.bias
        x = x * self.scale
        x = torch.sigmoid(x)
        x = self.group_norm(x)
        return x

batch_size = 128
in_channels = 8
out_channels = 32
height = width = 256
kernel_size = 3
num_groups = 8
bias_shape = (out_channels, 1, 1)
scale_shape = (out_channels, 1, 1)

def get_inputs():
    return [torch.rand(batch_size, in_channels, height, width)]

def get_init_inputs():
    return [in_channels, out_channels, kernel_size, num_groups, bias_shape, scale_shape]

Wait, I need to think more carefully. In NCHW, memory layout is: x[b, c, h, w] = ptr[((b * C + c) * H + h) * W + w] So if we fix b and c, elements with varying w are contiguous. For a given b and channel group, we want threads to load from the same channel but adjacent w, or same (h, w) but adjacent c. Actually, the best coalescing is when threads in a warp read contiguous memory locations. So if thread i reads channel c, same h, w = some_offset + i, then: address = ((b * C + c) * H + h) * W + some_offset + i These are contiguous addresses (stride 1), so this is perfectly coalesced. So in the group norm kernel, for a given (batch, group), we can view the data as 4 channels of 256x256 each. If we process it as one flat array of 262144 elements, we can have each warp load 32 contiguous elements. For a block of 256 threads (8 warps), if each thread needs to process 1024 elements, we can process the 262144 elements in 262144 / 256 = 1024 iterations. In iteration k: - thread tid loads element index: tid * 1 + k * 256 Wait, for contiguous access within a warp: - warp 0: threads 0-31 load addresses base + 0 to base + 31 - warp 1: threads 32-63 load addresses base + 32 to base + 63 - etc. So in iteration k: - all threads load global[idx = k * 256 + tid] This gives 256 contiguous floats per iteration, perfect coalescing. We do this for the first pass (sum and sum_squares), then reduce, then do it again for the normalization pass. But we need to be careful about numerical precision. For fp32, we'd accumulate in fp32. For the variance, the standard formula is: var = E[x^2] - (E[x])^2, but this can be numerically unstable. For group norm, PyTorch uses Welford's algorithm or the two-pass approach. Since we have only 262K elements and the values are post-sigmoid (bounded in [0, 1]), simple sum of squares minus mean squared should be fine. Actually, PyTorch's group norm typically uses the formula: mean = sum(x) / N var = sum((x - mean)^2) / N inv_std = rsqrt(var + eps) The `sum((x - mean)^2)` approach requires a second pass or loading all data twice. The `sum(x^2)/N - mean^2` approach only needs sum and sum of squares in the first pass, but the subtraction of nearly equal numbers can cause catastrophic cancellation. However, with sigmoid output in [0,1], this isn't too bad, but we should be careful. Actually, for better numerical accuracy matching PyTorch, we should probably do the two-pass approach or use Welford. But since the reference uses PyTorch's implementation, we need to match it within tolerance. PyTorch's GroupNorm uses the formula from the CUDA kernel: ``` mean = sum / count var = (sum_sq / count) - mean * mean if var < 0: var = 0 # (numerical issues) inv_std = rsqrt(var + eps) ``` Actually let me check. In PyTorch's native group norm CUDA kernel, it does: ```cpp mean = sum / N; var = sum_sq / N - mean * mean; if (var < 0) var = 0; invstd = rsqrt(var + eps); ``` Yes, PyTorch's internal group norm kernels often use the E[x^2] - E[x]^2 formula for efficiency, with a max(0, var) to handle negative values from numerical error. So we can safely use: sum_x = atomic or shared reduce sum_x2 = atomic or shared reduce mean = sum_x / N var = sum_x2 / N - mean * mean var = max(var, 0.0f) inv_std = rsqrtf(var + eps) Then normalize: (x - mean) * inv_std Wait, PyTorch's GroupNorm with affine=True also applies weight and bias: `y = (x - mean) * inv_std * gamma + beta` So we need to include gamma and beta from `self.group_norm.weight` and `self.group_norm.bias`. But these are parameters of the nn.GroupNorm module, learned during training. In ModelNew, we should initialize the same group_norm module and use its learned parameters in the forward kernel... wait, but the task says to produce numerically equivalent output. During inference (forward pass), these parameters are fixed. So in ModelNew.__init__, we should create the same nn.Conv2d, same bias/scale parameters, and nn.GroupNorm. Then in forward, instead of calling them sequentially, we use a custom kernel. For the custom kernel, we can pass: - input x - conv weight - conv bias (self.conv.bias, since nn.Conv2d has its own bias) - self.bias - self.scale - group_norm.weight - group_norm.bias Wait, the reference model uses `nn.Conv2d` which includes a bias term. So self.conv has weight + bias. Then it adds `self.bias`, multiplies by `self.scale`, applies sigmoid, then group_norm. So the full computation is: 1. conv(x) [with conv.bias] 2. + self.bias 3. * self.scale 4. sigmoid() 5. group_norm(with conv gn.weight, gn.bias) For the custom kernel, I can use PyTorch's `F.conv2d` for the conv, then a custom elementwise kernel. Or do the conv in CUDA too. Actually, doing the conv in CUDA gives more fusion opportunity. Let's think about a custom direct conv kernel. The input is 128x8x256x256. For each output pixel (b, c_out, h, w): output = sum_{c_in=0..7} sum_{kh=0..2} sum_{kw=0..2} input[b, c_in, h+kh-1, w+kw-1] * weight[c_out, c_in, kh, kw] + conv_bias[c_out] Then + bias[c_out], * scale[c_out], sigmoid For a custom kernel, tile by batch, output channel, and spatial. Actually, if each block uses 51KB, and we have 227KB per SM, we can fit at most 4 blocks per SM (4*51=204KB). With 256 threads per block, that's 1024 threads per SM. H100 supports up to 2048 threads per SM. So we'd get 50% occupancy due to SMEM. That might be OK for a memory-bound reduction, but for compute-bound conv, higher occupancy might help hide latency. Alternative: compute the conv, write to global memory, then run a group norm kernel. This allows more blocks per SM for the conv. But maybe we can still fuse everything with a different tiling strategy. Let me reconsider. What if we tile over output channels too? Like each block handles 8 output channels instead of 32. Then: - Weights: 8 * 8 * 3 * 3 = 576 floats = 2.3KB - Input: same, 10KB - Output intermediate: 8 * 16 * 16 = 2048 floats = 8KB - Total SMEM: ~20KB. Now, can we do group norm within the same block? Each block handles 8 channels and 256 spatial pixels. But group norm groups are 4 channels! So 8 channels = 2 groups. We need per-group stats over 4 channels × 16 × 16 = 1024 elements. But wait, group norm stats are over ALL spatial pixels (256x256), not just the 16x16 tile! Ah, this is the fundamental problem. Group norm needs global statistics per group (over the full 256x256 spatial extent). We can't compute group norm locally within a spatial tile unless we do multi-pass or atomic reductions. So the options for fusion are: 1. Single kernel but with atomics or workspace for partial stats 2. Separate conv and group norm kernels Given the complexity of global reductions in a conv kernel, I think a two-kernel approach is cleaner: - Kernel 1: Fused conv + bias + scale + sigmoid - Kernel 2: Group norm But we can maybe do a bit better. What about: - Kernel 1: Fused conv + bias + scale + sigmoid - But wait, we know the exact tile of group norm each block computes. Can we launch persistent blocks? Actually, let me think about a different architecture. Could we launch blocks where each block processes one (batch, group), computes ALL spatial pixels for that group (4 channels × 256 × 256)? That's 262144 elements per block. For the conv, each block would compute 4 output channels for all spatial locations. For 256 threads, each thread computes 1024 elements across 4 channels (or 256 elements per channel). That's 256 * 72 = 18432 MACs per thread! That's a lot of compute. But with no SMEM for output buffering, each thread loads input values from global memory. Input loading would not be reused across threads, leading to high memory traffic. Unless we stage input through SMEM tiles. Let's try: block processes 4 output channels for one batch item. We'll compute spatial tiles sequentially. - Load weights for 4 output channels: 4 * 8 * 9 = 288 floats = 1.15KB - For each spatial tile (say 32x32 = 1024 outputs): - Load input for 8 channels × 34 × 34 = 9248 floats = 37KB - Too much SMEM for the input tile! Hmm, 34x34 for 8 channels is 9248 floats. With 256 threads and shared memory, it's large but manageable (37KB). Plus weights (1KB) = 38KB. Fit 5-6 blocks per SM. OK. But then for 256x256 spatial, that's 8x8 = 64 tiles. Each block processes 64 tiles × 4 channels = total work is correct. After computing all tiles for 4 channels, we still need to do group norm. But the group norm stats are computed over all 262144 elements. We can compute partial sum/sum_sq per tile, accumulate them in SMEM, then at the end of all tiles, we have the stats. Then we need to go back and normalize all 262144 elements. But we don't have the elements stored! So we'd need to either: 1. Store all elements to global memory during the first pass, then reload for normalization 2. Recompute the conv for the normalization pass (expensive) 3. Have a two-phase approach within the block, but that requires either a lot of SMEM or global memory So it seems like writing to global memory is unavoidable for a clean group norm. Wait, what if we process the group in tiles, compute partial stats in registers, write to a workspace, sync across blocks, then a second kernel normalizes? That's: - Kernel 1: conv + write output + write partial stats per tile to workspace - Kernel 2: reduce partial stats, normalize output For spatial tiles of 16x16 and 4 channels per group, partial stats would be sum and sum_sq over 1024 elements per tile. There are 256/16 * 256/16 = 256 tiles per group. With 128 batches * 8 groups = 1024 groups, total partial stats = 1024 * 256 = 262144 partial sums. That's 262K pairs of floats = ~2MB workspace. Kernel 2 reads these partials (2MB) and the main output (268MB), writes normalized output (268MB). Actually, we can do even better. Kernel 2 can just read the partial stats, reduce them, then read the output and normalize. But wait, can we do the partial stat computation more efficiently? For each tile of 16x16 with 4 channels, while computing conv, we can accumulate sum and sum_sq in registers, then write to global workspace once per tile. Let me compare with the PyTorch approach: - PyTorch: Conv (reads 67MB, writes 268MB) → bias,scale,sigmoid (reads 268MB, writes 268MB) → group norm (reads 268MB, writes 268MB) with internal kernels - Total memory: ~1GB+ My two-kernel approach: - Kernel 1: Conv+bias+scale+sigmoid (reads 67MB, writes 268MB + 2MB workspace) - Kernel 2: Read output + partials, reduce, normalize, write (reads 268MB + 2MB, writes 268MB) - Total memory: ~673MB (about 60% of PyTorch) But PyTorch likely fuses bias+scale+sigmoid, and group_norm likely fuses its mean/var computation. The PyTorch memory traffic might be: - Conv: read 67MB, write 268MB - Fused elementwise: read 268MB, write 268MB - Group norm: read 268MB, write 268MB (but internal, so maybe partial reduction in SMEM) Total: ~1.1GB So my approach saves a good amount. But can we do better? What about a single kernel with true fusion using cooperative grid sync? Actually, on modern GPUs, we can use cooperative groups or grid synchronization for persistent kernels. But `cg::this_grid().sync()` requires the kernel to be launched with specific flags. In PyTorch `load_inline`, we can launch with `cudaLaunchCooperativeKernel` but it's tricky. Let me stick to the two-kernel approach for safety and optimization. Now let me design the kernels in detail. Kernel 1: Fused Conv + Bias + Scale + Sigmoid Design options: Option A: Each thread computes one output pixel for all 32 channels (2304 MACs). Block: 16x16 = 256 threads. Spatial tile: 16x16 = 256 pixels. Block outputs: 256 pixels * 32 channels = 8192 elements. SMEM: input (8*18*18=2592 floats), weights (32*8*9=2304 floats), total ~19.6KB. Regs per thread: 32 accumulators. This seems very solid. Register pressure is low (32 + a few temps). SMEM is moderate. Compute per thread is decent (2304 MACs). Option B: Each thread computes multiple output pixels for one channel. Block: 256 threads. Each warp (32 threads) handles 32 spatial pixels for one channel. 8 warps handle 8 channels. So block handles 8 channels, Spatial tile depends. I think Option A is cleaner because it gives good output channel parallelism and coalesced writes. Let me detail Option A: Grid: - dim3 grid(batch_size, // 128 (H + 15) / 16, // 16 (W + 15) / 16); // 16 Block: - dim3 block(16, 16); // 256 threads Thread mapping: - threadIdx.x -> w within tile (0-15) - threadIdx.y -> h within tile (0-15) Outputs per thread: all 32 channels for pixel (h, w) in the tile. SMEM: ```cuda __shared__ float smem_input[8 * 18 * 18]; // 2592 __shared__ float smem_weight[32 * 8 * 9]; // 2304 ``` Loading weights: ```cuda for (int i = threadIdx.x + threadIdx.y * 16; i < 32*8*9; i += 256) { smem_weight[i] = weight[i]; } ``` Loading input: Input tile covers (tile_h * 16 - 1) to (tile_h * 16 + 16) in h, and similarly in w. But we need to handle padding. Actually, for simplicity, I'll handle padding by checking bounds. For each channel c_in: for (int ih = 0; ih < 18; ih++) { for (int iw = 0; iw < 18; iw++) { // global h = tile_h * 16 + ih - 1 // global w = tile_w * 16 + iw - 1 // load if in bounds, else 0 } } With 256 threads, we have 2592 loads. 2592 / 256 = 10.125. So some threads load 10, some 11. That's fine. Wait, if we launch 256 threads and need 2592 loads, we can do: ``` int tid = threadIdx.x + threadIdx.y * blockDim.x; for (int idx = tid; idx < 8 * 18 * 18; idx += 256) { int c_in = idx / (18 * 18); int rem = idx % (18 * 18); int ih = rem / 18; int iw = rem % 18; int h = blockIdx.y * 16 + ih - 1; int w = blockIdx.z * 16 + iw - 1; float val = 0.0f; if (h >= 0 && h < H && w >= 0 && w < W) { val = input[((blockIdx.x * C_in + c_in) * H + h) * W + w]; } smem_input[idx] = val; } ``` Then after __syncthreads(), each thread computes for its (threadIdx.y, threadIdx.x) within the tile: ``` out_h = blockIdx.y * 16 + threadIdx.y; out_w = blockIdx.z * 16 + threadIdx.x; if (out_h < H && out_w < W) { for (int c_out = 0; c_out < 32; c_out++) { float acc = conv_bias[c_out]; for (int c_in = 0; c_in < 8; c_in++) { for (int kh = 0; kh < 3; kh++) { for (int kw = 0; kw < 3; kw++) { float in_val = smem_input[(c_in * 18 + (threadIdx.y + kh)) * 18 + (threadIdx.x + kw)]; float w_val = smem_weight[((c_out * 8 + c_in) * 3 + kh) * 3 + kw]; acc += in_val * w_val; } } } acc = acc + bias[c_out]; acc = acc * scale[c_out]; acc = 1.0f / (1.0f + expf(-acc)); // sigmoid // Write to global memory output[((blockIdx.x * 32 + c_out) * H + out_h) * W + out_w] = acc; // Also accumulate group norm stats... if we wanted to } } ``` Wait, each thread does 32 * 8 * 9 = 2304 MACs, plus loads from SMEM. But the inner loops are fully unrollable? The compiler might not fully unroll 3x3 loops but 32 and 8 should be unrolled. Actually, if we write it as loops, the compiler will decide. We can add `#pragma unroll` to help. For the output write pattern: - Thread (0, 0) writes to all 32 channels for spatial pixel (0,0): addresses = [b*32*HW + c*HW + 0], strided by HW between channels. This is NOT coalesced! Each write has stride 65536 floats = 256KB. - Adjacent threads write to adjacent spatial pixels but for the SAME channel: thread (0,0) and thread (0,1) write channel 0 at w=0 and w=1, which ARE adjacent. So if we write channel by channel, for a fixed c_out, all 256 threads write to adjacent spatial pixels within the tile. That's coalesced! But the channels are written sequentially. Wait, actually: output[((blockIdx.x * 32 + c_out) * H + out_h) * W + out_w] For fixed c_out, adjacent threads (same out_h, adjacent out_w) write to adjacent memory. Good coalescing. But the loop is over c_out from 0 to 31. We do 32 coalesced writes per thread. Each write is 256 bytes for the warp. That's good. We could also reorder so threads collaborate, but the above is probably fine. For numerical accuracy: we're using fp32, and the operations are straightforward. Let me think about potential issues: 1. Padding: We handle it by loading 0 for out-of-bounds. 2. Thread divergence: The `if (out_h < H && out_w < W)` check. For tiles fully inside, all threads participate. For boundary tiles on the right/bottom, some threads may be inactive. With 256x256 and tile size 16, only the last row/column might have this. Actually, 256 is divisible by 16, so all tiles are full! No boundary issues except the -1 padding in input. Easy! 3. SMEM bank conflicts: The input is accessed as `smem_input[c_in * 324 + (threadIdx.y + kh) * 18 + (threadIdx.x + kw)]`. For a fixed c_in and kh, adjacent threads (varying threadIdx.x) read adjacent addresses (stride 1). That's good. For different kh, we offset by 18, which might cause some conflicts if multiple threads in a warp hit the same bank, but with 32 banks and stride of 1 in the inner dimension, it should be mostly OK. Now, for Kernel 2: Group Norm We need an efficient group norm kernel. Let's think about the design. Input: [128, 32, 256, 256] (output from kernel 1) Groups: 8, so 4 channels per group. Output: same shape. For each (batch, group): - Mean and var over 4 * 256 * 256 = 262144 elements - Then normalize: (x - mean) * inv_std * gamma + beta We can implement this as: - Grid: [batch_size, num_groups] = [128, 8] blocks - Block: 256 or 512 threads Wait, 128 * 8 = 1024 blocks total. With ~100 SMs, that's only ~10 blocks per SM. Each block needs to handle 262144 elements. With 256 threads, each thread handles 1024 elements. That's a decent amount of work per thread. Approach for each block: 1. Each thread loads its subset of elements in strided fashion 2. Compute sum and sum_sq in registers 3. Block reduce sum and sum_sq using shared memory 4. Compute mean, var, inv_std 5. Reload elements and normalize, write back For the data layout: [N, C, H, W] = [128, 32, 256, 256] For batch b and group g, the channels are [4g, 4g+1, 4g+2, 4g+3]. Elements are at: base + ((b * 32 + 4g + c) * 256 + h) * 256 + w for c in [0,1,2,3] and h,w in [0,255]. If we treat this as a flat array of 262144 elements for the block, we can use: idx = tid + block_offset But the layout isn't contiguous across channels for a single spatial pixel. Actually it is: for a given (b, h, w), channels c and c+1 are adjacent. But here we iterate over all channels and all spatial pixels. The most cache-friendly access would be to process the 4 channels for each spatial pixel, or do spatial pixels first. If we linearize as: flat_idx = c * (256*256) + h * 256 + w where c in [0,3], then: memory address = base + (b * 32 + 4g + c) * 65536 + h * 256 + w = base + (b * 32 + 4g) * 65536 + c * 65536 + h * 256 + w So within a group, the 4 channels are separated by 65536 floats (256KB). That's a huge stride. If adjacent threads read different flat_idx values: - flat_idx 0: c=0, h=0, w=0 - flat_idx 1: c=0, h=0, w=1 ... - flat_idx 255: c=0, h=0, w=255 - flat_idx 256: c=0, h=1, w=0 For 262144 elements, if we have 256 threads and each thread reads every 256th element: thread tid reads: tid, tid+256, tid+512, ... For tid=0: flat_idx 0, 256, 512, ... which are (c=0,h=0,w=0), (c=0,h=1,w=0), (c=0,h=2,w=0), ... Memory addresses: base + 0, base + 256, base + 512, ... strided by 256 floats = 1KB. Not contiguous within a warp, but adjacent threads read addresses that are 1KB apart. That's 32-way bank conflict for L1, but coalescing? Actually, for global memory, the address pattern matters. If thread 0 reads addr A, thread 1 reads addr A+1KB, thread 2 reads A+2KB... this is strided by 1024 bytes. A warp reads 32 addresses spaced 1KB apart. This might not be coalesced well. Actually, for A100/H100, coalescing means threads in a warp should read a contiguous 128-byte segment. Here, threads read spread-out addresses, so each warp issues many separate transactions. A better pattern: have threads read contiguous elements. If we want each of the 256 threads to process 1024 elements, total = 262144. We can process in tiles. In each iteration i: base_flat = i * 256 thread tid reads flat_idx = base_flat + tid For base_flat = 0, thread 0 reads flat_idx 0 (addr X), thread 1 reads flat_idx 1 (addr X+4), ..., thread 255 reads flat_idx 255 (addr X+1020). These are contiguous! Then base_flat = 256, thread 0 reads flat_idx 256 (addr X+1024), etc. With 1024 iterations (each handling 256 elements), we process 262144 elements. Memory layout of flat_idx: flat_idx 0-65535: c=0 flat_idx 65536-131071: c=1 flat_idx 131072-196607: c=2 flat_idx 196608-262143: c=3 Wait, if we define flat_idx this way, then: - flat_idx 0: (c=0, h=0, w=0) - flat_idx 1: (c=0, h=0, w=1) ... - flat_idx 65535: (c=0, h=255, w=255) - flat_idx 65536: (c=1, h=0, w=0) And memory address for flat_idx: If flat_idx < 65536: c = 0, remainder = flat_idx If flat_idx < 131072: c = 1, remainder = flat_idx - 65536 etc. Memory address = base + (b*32 + 4g + c) * 65536 + remainder So for flat_idx 0 to 255 (all c=0, h=0, w=0 to 255): addresses = base + (b*32 + 4g) * 65536 + 0 to 255 These are contiguous! Great coalescing. For flat_idx 256 to 511 (c=0, h=1, w=0 to 255): addresses = base + (b*32 + 4g) * 65536 + 256 to 511 Also contiguous. But when we cross from flat_idx 65535 to 65536: flat_idx 65535: c=0, h=255, w=255 → addr = base + (b*32 + 4g) * 65536 + 65535 flat_idx 65536: c=1, h=0, w=0 → addr = base + (b*32 + 4g + 1) * 65536 + 0 Gap of 1 element (4 bytes) between these! So the c=1 data is immediately adjacent to c=0 data in memory! Wait, is that right? Let me check: Channel 0 ends at: base + (b*32 + 4g) * 65536 + 65535 Channel 1 starts at: base + (b*32 + 4g + 1) * 65536 + 0 Difference = 65536 - 65535 = 1 element! OH! In NCHW, the channels ARE adjacent in memory! Channel c ends at offset (c+1)*HW - 1, and channel c+1 starts at offset (c+1)*HW. They are contiguous! So for our group (channels 4g, 4g+1, 4g+2, 4g+3), the memory is fully contiguous! 4 * 65536 = 262144 contiguous floats. That's fantastic! So a (batch, group) corresponds to exactly 262144 contiguous floats in memory. Address range: base + (b*32 + 4g) * 65536 to base + (b*32 + 4g + 4) * 65536 - 1 So if block (b, g) processes this contiguous range, and threads read contiguous elements within it, we get perfect coalescing. Implementation: ```cuda __global__ void group_norm_kernel(float* in, float* out, float* gamma, float* beta, int N, int C, int H, int W, int num_groups, float eps) { int b = blockIdx.x; int g = blockIdx.y; int tid = threadIdx.x; int group_size = C / num_groups; int group_elems = group_size * H * W; float* group_ptr = in + ((b * C + g * group_size) * H * W); float* out_ptr = out + ((b * C + g * group_size) * H * W); // First pass: compute sum and sum_sq float sum = 0.0f; float sum_sq = 0.0f; for (int idx = tid; idx < group_elems; idx += blockDim.x) { float val = group_ptr[idx]; sum += val; sum_sq += val * val; } // Block reduction __shared__ float shared_sum[256]; __shared__ float shared_sum_sq[256]; shared_sum[tid] = sum; shared_sum_sq[tid] = sum_sq; __syncthreads(); // Reduce (can use warp shuffle) for (int offset = blockDim.x / 2; offset > 0; offset /= 2) { if (tid < offset) { shared_sum[tid] += shared_sum[tid + offset]; shared_sum_sq[tid] += shared_sum_sq[tid + offset]; } __syncthreads(); } float mean = shared_sum[0] / group_elems; float var = shared_sum_sq[0] / group_elems - mean * mean; if (var < 0) var = 0; float inv_std = rsqrtf(var + eps); __syncthreads(); // Second pass: normalize for (int idx = tid; idx < group_elems; idx += blockDim.x) { int c_offset = idx / (H * W); int c = g * group_size + c_offset; float val = group_ptr[idx]; float norm = (val - mean) * inv_std; norm = norm * gamma[c] + beta[c]; out_ptr[idx] = norm; } } ``` Wait, the gamma and beta indexing. In PyTorch GroupNorm: - gamma and beta are per-channel: shape [C] - c ranges from g*group_size to (g+1)*group_size - 1 But `idx` in the contiguous block: idx 0 to 65535: c_offset = 0, c = g*group_size idx 65536 to 131071: c_offset = 1, c = g*group_size + 1 So `c_offset = idx / (H * W)` gives us which channel within the group. Then `c = g * group_size + c_offset`. This works! Performance considerations: - blockDim.x = 256 seems good - Each thread does 262144/256 = 1024 iterations in each pass - In each iteration, single load. So 1024 loads per thread per pass - Bank conflicts in SMEM reduction? We use 256 floats, all threads write to their index. Reduction uses halving offsets. With 256 being a power of 2, and warp size 32, the first few steps have all active threads in each warp, no divergent warps. Should be OK. No wait, in the reduction, `if (tid < offset)` causes thread divergence for warps where some threads are < offset and some >=. But actually, for offset >= 32, all threads in a warp satisfy or don't satisfy the condition together (since warp handles 32 contiguous tids). For offset < 32, threads in a warp DO diverge. But warp shuffle is faster for the last few reductions anyway. We can optimize with warp shuffles: ```cuda // Warp reduction for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset /= 2) { sum += __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, sum, offset); sum_sq += __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, sum_sq, offset); } if (tid % 32 == 0) { shared_sum[tid / 32] = sum; shared_sum_sq[tid / 32] = sum_sq; } __syncthreads(); // Then reduce across warps in shared memory if (tid < 32) { sum = (tid < blockDim.x / 32) ? shared_sum[tid] : 0; sum_sq = (tid < blockDim.x / 32) ? shared_sum_sq[tid] : 0; for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset /= 2) { sum += __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, sum, offset); sum_sq += __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, sum_sq, offset); } if (tid == 0) { shared_sum[0] = sum; shared_sum_sq[0] = sum_sq; } } ``` This is more efficient. With blockDim.x = 256, we have 8 warps. The warp shuffle reduction is very fast. For H100, should we use more threads? Like 512 or 1024? 256 threads per block gives 1024 blocks total. On 100+ SMs, each SM gets about 10 blocks. With 256 threads, that's about 2560 threads per SM, well under the 2048 limit. Actually 10 blocks * 256 threads = 2560 > 2048, so occupancy is limited to 8 blocks per SM = 2048 threads. Still fine. Each thread does 1024 iterations × 2 passes = 2048 loads. At memory bandwidth of 3.35TB/s, and 268MB data read twice + written once = 804MB. At 3.35TB/s that's 0.24ms. But we also have 2MB for partial stats or just the group norm itself. Wait, for the two-pass group norm, we read all elements twice and write once: 3 * 268MB = 804MB. Plus conv kernel reads 67MB + writes 268MB = 335MB. Total ~1140MB. At 3.35TB/s = 0.34ms. But compute will add some time. PyTorch likely has similar or higher memory traffic. But wait, I think we can potentially avoid one read. In the group norm kernel, we read elements, compute stats, then read AGAIN to normalize. Can we keep elements in registers? No, 1024 elements per thread is too much. Can we use a tile-based approach where a block processes a tile, computes partial stats, writes normalized result, and accumulates partial stats? Then we have partial stats per block that need reduction. This requires either atomics, a second kernel, or grid sync. Actually, can we use a single warp-shuffle tree for the entire reduction across all threads, and then have the normalization happen without reloading? No, because we don't know the mean until all elements are processed. But we COULD have each thread batch its elements: process 32 elements, store in registers, compute sum and sum_sq, then at the end of all elements, reduce, then normalize those 32 elements and write them. Then process the next 32. Wait, that's brilliant! Each thread can process in chunks: For chunk_idx from 0 to num_chunks-1: int base = chunk_idx * chunk_size; // Load chunk_size elements into registers float vals[32]; // chunk_size = 32 for i = 0 to 31: vals[i] = group_ptr[base + i]; // or strided access // accumulate stats for i... sum += vals[i], sum_sq += vals[i]*vals[i] // reduce stats across block // compute mean, inv_std // Then process chunks again: for chunk_idx from 0 to num_chunks-1: // reload the SAME elements? We lost them from registers! ``` No wait, we can't keep all chunks in registers. Unless we recompute or reload. What if we do: process a chunk, accumulate stats across the block, and if the block were small enough that all elements fit in SMEM, we could store in SMEM and then normalize. But 262K floats per block = 1MB, too large for SMEM. Alternative: process chunks, accumulate global stats, write elements to global memory. Then after stats are computed, reload and normalize. That's the two-pass approach. Or: process ALL elements for stats (one pass), then normalize (second pass). Two global memory passes. Actually, what if we use persistent threads? The block computes stats, then loops through the elements again to normalize. That's what I wrote. The elements are read twice. Is there a way to read only once? Yes, if we use the partial-stat tile approach: - Divide group_elems into tiles of say 4096 elements (16 warps * 256 elements per warp?). Wait, let me think about tile size. - Tile size = blockDim.x * unroll_factor. Say 256 * 16 = 4096. - Each block handles multiple tiles of the SAME (batch, group). - For each tile, threads load their elements, store in local registers (16 per thread), compute partial sum/sum_sq, write elements to global memory (or maybe keep in SMEM if tile fits)... but SMEM for 4096 floats = 16KB. Plus the reduction workspace is small. So we can store one tile in SMEM! - SMEM: 4096 floats for the tile data = 16KB - Compute partial sum/sum_sq on the fly - Write tile data to global memory (or keep in SMEM if we have enough for the whole group... no, 1MB too much) - Actually, we don't need to write immediately. We can process all tiles for stats accumulation first. Wait, I think we can do: 1. First pass over all tiles: compute partial sum and sum_sq per tile, store partials in global workspace 2. Reduce all partials to mean and inv_std (could be done in the same kernel if we process all tiles first, but then we need to know when all blocks are done) 3. Second pass: load data, normalize using the now-known mean/inv_std This still requires two passes over the data. The only advantage is that we compute partial stats in fast local registers before writing to workspace. But the dominant cost is global memory bandwidth, and this doesn't reduce memory reads. Actually, wait. Can we atomically accumulate sum and sum_sq? If all blocks use atomicAdd on two floats per (batch, group), we can compute stats in one pass. Then a second pass normalizes. This is: - Kernel: each block processes a tile, computes local sum/sum_sq, atomically adds to global mean/var accumulator. Also writes the elements to memory. - All blocks finish. - Then normalization kernel reads elements and applies normalization. Wait, but atomicAdd on fp32 to global memory is very slow for 1024 groups × many blocks contending. A better approach: each block writes partial sum and sum_sq for its tile to a global workspace. The number of tiles per group = 262144 / 4096 = 64 tiles. 64 partials per group × 1024 groups = 65536 partials. Each partial is 2 floats = 8 bytes. Total workspace: 512KB. Then a second kernel reduces these partials. So: Kernel 1a: compute conv + elementwise, write output, and also compute partial stats per tile. But this complicates the conv kernel with group norm logic. Maybe separate is cleaner. Actually, I just realized: what if we compute the group norm IN PLACE, and use a clever approach where we process the group in two halves, using registers more aggressively? For group norm, PyTorch's CUDA implementation uses either: 1. A warp-level reduction per row (for LayerNorm-like cases) 2. A block-level reduction across all elements Since we have 262K elements per group (quite a lot), PyTorch might use a multi-block reduction for group norm. For group norm in PyTorch with N=128, C=32, H=W=256: - It likely launches a kernel to compute mean and inv_std, storing them per group - Then another kernel to normalize Actually, looking at PyTorch source, native_group_norm typically uses: 1. compute_mean_var: probably one block per (batch, group), each thread loads elements and reduces 2. forward: uses the precomputed mean/var to normalize Given that we're matching a reference that uses PyTorch's built-in, and PyTorch's built-in is highly optimized, to beat it we mainly need to: 1. Fuse the elementwise ops with conv to save memory bandwidth 2. Keep group norm competitive I think the two-kernel approach is a solid win from the fused conv alone. Actually, I should also check: does PyTorch have conv+bias fusion? `nn.Conv2d` includes bias, and cudnn convolutions can fuse bias with the conv. Yes, cudnn convolutions typically fuse conv+bias into a single kernel. So PyTorch's conv already includes the conv bias in one kernel. Then `x = x + self.bias` would be a separate kernel. Then `x = x * self.scale` would ideally be fused with the add, but in eager mode without graph optimization, it might be a separate kernel. Kernel 1: Fused Conv + Bias + Scale + Sigmoid I'll write it with explicit shared memory loading and `#pragma unroll` for performance. I'll also make sure the input loading is efficient. One concern: with block size 16x16 and each thread computing 32 output channels, I have 32 nested loops. To ensure the compiler unrolls efficiently, we can use template parameters or manually unroll critical loops. But 32 channels is a loop that the compiler might not fully unroll due to instruction cache pressure. We can try `#pragma unroll` or process channels in chunks. Let me think: 32 * 8 * 9 = 2304 multiplications. If the compiler unrolls the outer loop over 32 channels, that's 32 copies of an 8*9 inner loop. That's a lot of instructions but should fit in instruction cache (~32KB). Each inner iteration is a few instructions. Actually, `#pragma unroll` on the c_out loop with 32 iterations adds 32x code size. The inner body is ~15 instructions. 32*15 = 480 instructions per c_in loop. For 8 c_in: 3840 instructions. That's a lot but manageable. However, compiler might refuse to unroll 32. Alternative: process 4 channels at a time, unroll by 4. ```cuda #pragma unroll for (int c_out_base = 0; c_out_base < 32; c_out_base += 4) { float acc0 = conv_bias[c_out_base + 0]; float acc1 = conv_bias[c_out_base + 1]; float acc2 = conv_bias[c_out_base + 2]; float acc3 = conv_bias[c_out_base + 3]; #pragma unroll for (int c_in = 0; c_in < 8; c_in++) { #pragma unroll for (int kh = 0; kh < 3; kh++) { #pragma unroll for (int kw = 0; kw < 3; kw++) { float v = smem_input[...]; acc0 += v * sw[0]; acc1 += v * sw[1]; acc2 += v * sw[2]; acc3 += v * sw[3]; } } } // elementwise acc0 = sigmoid((acc0 + bias[c_out_base+0]) * scale[c_out_base+0]); // etc. write to memory } ``` This processes 4 channels per outer iteration, so only 8 outer iterations. Each iteration unrolls 3*3=9 innerMACs, times 4 channels = 36 MACs. With 8 iterations = 288MACs per c_in. Total outer iterations = 8 c_in * 8 channel_groups = 64. More manageable. Wait, actually the weight indexing needs to be right. Let me structure this more carefully. Actually, to maximize ILP (instruction-level parallelism), we should process independent accumulators. Having 4 or 8 independent accumulators is good. Let me structure it as processing 8 channels per outer block: ```cuda float acc[8]; #pragma unroll for (int i = 0; i < 8; i++) { acc[i] = conv_bias[c_out_base + i]; } #pragma unroll for (int c_in = 0; c_in < 8; c_in++) { #pragma unroll for (int kh = 0; kh < 3; kh++) { #pragma unroll for (int kw = 0; kw < 3; kw++) { float v = smem_input[(c_in * 18 + (y + kh)) * 18 + (x + kw)]; #pragma unroll for (int i = 0; i < 8; i++) { acc[i] += v * smem_weight[((c_out_base + i) * 8 + c_in) * 9 + kh * 3 + kw]; } } } } #pragma unroll for (int i = 0; i < 8; i++) { int c = c_out_base + i; acc[i] = (acc[i] + bias[c]) * scale[c]; acc[i] = 1.0f / (1.0f + expf(-acc[i])); output[...] = acc[i]; } ``` With c_out_base in {0, 8, 16, 24}, we have 4 outer iterations. Each iteration loads the same input value `v` and reuses it for 8 MACs. This is very efficient! Input loading is amortized. Total independent accumulators: 8. Good ILP. Now, c_out_base processing order: if we do 0, 8, 16, 24, the writes for a given spatial pixel are: - iteration 0: write channels 0-7 - iteration 1: write channels 8-15 etc. Each write for a fixed channel group is coalesced across threads. This is a great structure. Now, for the output write, we need to be careful about coalescing. For channels c_out_base + i at spatial (h, w): address = ((b * 32 + c_out_base + i) * H + h) * W + w If we write for each `i`, the addresses for adjacent threads (different `w` but same `h`, `i`) are: - Thread (x,y) and thread (x+1,y): addresses differ by 1 - But for fixed thread and varying i: addresses differ by H*W = 65536 So for each `i`, the write within a warp is coalesced (threads write adjacent w). And we do 32 total writes per thread (4 iterations * 8 channels). This is good. For the elementwise ops before writing, we might also compute partial group norm stats but we decided not to. Kernel 2: Group Norm Let me write an optimized version using warp shuffle. ```cuda __global__ void group_norm_kernel( const float* __restrict__ in, float* __restrict__ out, const float* __restrict__ gamma, const float* __restrict__ beta, int N, int C, int H, int W, int num_groups, float eps ) { int b = blockIdx.x; int g = blockIdx.y; int tid = threadIdx.x; int group_size = C / num_groups; int group_elems = group_size * H * W; const float* g_in = in + ((b * C + g * group_size) * H * W); float* g_out = out + ((b * C + g * group_size) * H * W); float sum = 0.0f; float sum_sq = 0.0f; // Coalesced strided load for (int idx = tid; idx < group_elems; idx += blockDim.x) { float v = g_in[idx]; sum += v; sum_sq += v * v; } // Warp shuffle reduction #pragma unroll for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset /= 2) { sum += __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, sum, offset); sum_sq += __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, sum_sq, offset); } __shared__ float sh_sum[32]; __shared__ float sh_sum_sq[32]; int lane = tid % 32; int warp_id = tid / 32; if (lane == 0) { sh_sum[warp_id] = sum; sh_sum_sq[warp_id] = sum_sq; } __syncthreads(); if (tid < 8) { // 256 threads / 32 = 8 warps sum = sh_sum[tid]; sum_sq = sh_sum_sq[tid]; #pragma unroll for (int offset = 4; offset > 0; offset /= 2) { sum += __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, sum, offset); sum_sq += __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, sum_sq, offset); } if (tid == 0) { sh_sum[0] = sum; sh_sum_sq[0] = sum_sq; } } __syncthreads(); float mean = sh_sum[0] / group_elems; float var = sh_sum_sq[0] / group_elems - mean * mean; if (var < 0.0f) var = 0.0f; float inv_std = rsqrtf(var + eps); // Normalize for (int idx = tid; idx < group_elems; idx += blockDim.x) { int c_offset = idx / (H * W); int c = g * group_size + c_offset; float v = g_in[idx]; float norm = (v - mean) * inv_std; norm = norm * gamma[c] + beta[c]; g_out[idx] = norm; } } ``` This should be quite efficient. Let me double check: - First load pass: 1024 iterations per thread (262144/256). Each iteration one load, 2 FMAs. - Warp reduce: fast - Shared reduce across 8 warps: 8 threads participate, shuffle-based - Second pass: 1024 iterations per thread. Each iteration one load, a few arithmetic ops, one store. Wait, each thread does 1024 iterations. Each iteration touches 1 float = 4 bytes. Per pass: 1024 * 4 * 256 threads = 1MB per block (the full group). But we read all groups. Total memory: - First pass load: 128 * 8 * 1MB = 1024MB read - Second pass load: 1024MB read - Second pass store: 1024MB write Total for group norm: 3GB! That can't be right. Wait, group_elems = 4 * 256 * 256 = 262144 floats = 1MB per (batch, group). Number of (batch, group) = 128 * 8 = 1024. Total data = 1024 * 1MB = 1GB. First pass: each thread loads some elements. All threads across all blocks together load the entire 1GB once. Second pass: load the entire 1GB again. Third: write the entire 1GB. Total: 3GB for group norm. Earlier I said 268MB output, but actually 128 * 32 * 256 * 256 * 4 bytes = 1GB. Yes! I made an error earlier. Let me recalculate: - batch_size = 128 - out_channels = 32 - height = width = 256 Total output elements = 128 * 32 * 256 * 256 = 268,435,456 At 4 bytes per float = 1,073,741,824 bytes = 1GB. So: - Conv input: 128 * 8 * 256 * 256 * 4 = 256MB - Conv output (intermediate): 1GB - Group norm output: 1GB Memory traffic for PyTorch reference: 1. Conv: read 256MB input + weights(~9KB) + conv_bias(~128B), write 1GB output: ~1.25GB 2. Add bias: read 1GB, write 1GB: 2GB 3. Mul scale: read 1GB, write 1GB: 2GB 4. Sigmoid: read 1GB, write 1GB: 2GB 5. Group norm: read 1GB, write 1GB (possibly more for internal): ~2-3GB Total PyTorch: easily 8-10GB of memory traffic! At 3.35TB/s, that's 2.4-3ms just for memory. My fused kernel 1: read 256MB input, write 1GB output. = 1.25GB My group norm kernel: read 1GB twice, write 1GB once. = 3GB Total: 4.25GB That's a huge reduction! Very promising. In eager mode, yes: ```python x = x + self.bias # kernel 1 x = x * self.scale # kernel 2 x = torch.sigmoid(x) # kernel 3 ``` Unless PyTorch's executor does some fusion, which it typically doesn't in eager mode without a graph. Now let me also consider: can we make the group norm kernel do in-place normalization to save memory? Yes, we can write to the same buffer. In the kernel, `out` can be the same pointer as `in`. PyTorch's sigmoid uses a numerically stable implementation that avoids overflow. For fp32 and typical values from conv, direct `expf(-x)` should be fine as long as we clamp or use `fminf/fmaxf`. But PyTorch's sigmoid handles edge cases specially. Actually, PyTorch's sigmoid implementation for CUDA: ```cpp // From aten/src/ATen/native/cuda/Activation.cu z = 1 / (1 + std::exp(-z)); ``` With some special handling for large negative values? Actually, `expf(1000)` overflows to inf, so `1/(1+inf) = 0` which is correct for sigmoid. `expf(-1000)` underflows to 0, so `1/(1+0) = 1`, correct. So `1.0f / (1.0f + expf(-x))` is numerically stable enough for fp32. However, for large positive x, `-x` is very negative, `expf(-x)` underflows to 0, result is 1. Good. For large negative x, `-x` is very positive, `expf(-x)` overflows to inf, `1+inf = inf`, `1/inf = 0`. Good. So the naive formula is actually fine. OK let me draft the Python code. ```python import torch import torch.nn as nn from torch.utils.cpp_extension import load_inline cuda_source = ''' #include <torch/extension.h> #include <cuda_runtime.h> __global__ void fused_conv_bias_scale_sigmoid_kernel( const float* __restrict__ input, const float* __restrict__ weight, const float* __restrict__ conv_bias, const float* __restrict__ bias, const float* __restrict__ scale, float* __restrict__ output, int N, int C_in, int C_out, int H, int W, int H_out, int W_out ) { // Block: 16x16 threads // Grid: N x ((H_out+15)/16) x ((W_out+15)/16) int bx = blockIdx.x; // batch int by = blockIdx.y; // tile h int bz = blockIdx.z; // tile w int tx = threadIdx.x; // 0-15, w within tile int ty = threadIdx.y; // 0-15, h within tile int out_h = by * 16 + ty; int out_w = bz * 16 + tx; if (out_h >= H_out || out_w >= W_out) return; // Shared memory __shared__ float smem_input[8 * 18 * 18]; __shared__ float smem_weight[32 * 8 * 9]; int tid = ty * 16 + tx; // Load weights (32*8*9 = 2304) #pragma unroll for (int i = tid; i < 32 * 8 * 9; i += 256) { smem_weight[i] = weight[i]; } // Load input tile (8 * 18 * 18 = 2592) int tile_h = by * 16; int tile_w = bz * 16; #pragma unroll for (int i = tid; i < 8 * 18 * 18; i += 256) { int c_in = i / (18 * 18); int rem = i % (18 * 18); int ih = rem / 18; int iw = rem % 18; int h = tile_h + ih - 1; int w = tile_w + iw - 1; float val = 0.0f; if (h >= 0 && h < H && w >= 0 && w < W) { val = input[((bx * C_in + c_in) * H + h) * W + w]; } smem_input[i] = val; } __syncthreads(); int in_y = ty + 1; int in_x = tx + 1; // Process output channels in groups of 8 #pragma unroll for (int c_out_base = 0; c_out_base < 32; c_out_base += 8) { float acc[8]; #pragma unroll for (int i = 0; i < 8; i++) { acc[i] = conv_bias[c_out_base + i]; } #pragma unroll for (int c_in = 0; c_in < 8; c_in++) { #pragma unroll for (int kh = 0; kh < 3; kh++) { #pragma unroll for (int kw = 0; kw < 3; kw++) { float v = smem_input[(c_in * 18 + (in_y + kh - 1)) * 18 + (in_x + kw - 1)]; #pragma unroll for (int i = 0; i < 8; i++) { acc[i] += v * smem_weight[((c_out_base + i) * 8 + c_in) * 9 + kh * 3 + kw]; } } } } #pragma unroll for (int i = 0; i < 8; i++) { int c = c_out_base + i; float val = (acc[i] + bias[c]) * scale[c]; val = 1.0f / (1.0f + expf(-val)); output[((bx * C_out + c) * H_out + out_h) * W_out + out_w] = val; } } } __global__ void group_norm_kernel( const float* __restrict__ in, float* __restrict__ out, const float* __restrict__ gamma, const float* __restrict__ beta, int N, int C, int H, int W, int num_groups, float eps ) { int b = blockIdx.x; int g = blockIdx.y; int tid = threadIdx.x; int group_size = C / num_groups; int group_elems = group_size * H * W; int channel_start = g * group_size; const float* g_in = in + ((b * C + channel_start) * H * W); float* g_out = out + ((b * C + channel_start) * H * W); float sum = 0.0f; float sum_sq = 0.0f; // Coalesced strided load for (int idx = tid; idx < group_elems; idx += blockDim.x) { float v = __ldg(g_in + idx); sum += v; sum_sq += v * v; } // Warp shuffle reduction #pragma unroll for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset /= 2) { sum += __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, sum, offset); sum_sq += __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, sum_sq, offset); } __shared__ float sh_sum[8]; __shared__ float sh_sum_sq[8]; int lane = tid % 32; int warp_id = tid / 32; int num_warps = blockDim.x / 32; // 8 if (lane == 0 && warp_id < num_warps) { sh_sum[warp_id] = sum; sh_sum_sq[warp_id] = sum_sq; } __syncthreads(); if (tid < num_warps) { sum = sh_sum[tid]; sum_sq = sh_sum_sq[tid]; #pragma unroll for (int offset = num_warps / 2; offset > 0; offset /= 2) { sum += __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, sum, offset); sum_sq += __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, sum_sq, offset); } if (tid == 0) { sh_sum[0] = sum; sh_sum_sq[0] = sum_sq; } } __syncthreads(); float mean = sh_sum[0] / group_elems; float var = sh_sum_sq[0] / group_elems - mean * mean; if (var < 0.0f) var = 0.0f; float inv_std = rsqrtf(var + eps); // Normalize and write for (int idx = tid; idx < group_elems; idx += blockDim.x) { int c_offset = idx / (H * W); int c = channel_start + c_offset; float v = g_in[idx]; float norm = (v - mean) * inv_std; norm = norm * gamma[c] + beta[c]; g_out[idx] = norm; } } torch::Tensor fused_forward( torch::Tensor input, torch::Tensor weight, torch::Tensor conv_bias, torch::Tensor bias, torch::Tensor scale, torch::Tensor gn_weight, torch::Tensor gn_bias, int num_groups ) { int N = input.size(0); int C_in = input.size(1); int H = input.size(2); int W = input.size(3); int C_out = weight.size(0); int H_out = H - 2; // kernel_size=3, padding=0 int W_out = W - 2; auto output = torch::empty({N, C_out, H_out, W_out}, input.options()); dim3 block(16, 16); dim3 grid(N, (H_out + 15) / 16, (W_out + 15) / 16); fused_conv_bias_scale_sigmoid_kernel<<<grid, block>>>( input.data_ptr<float>(), weight.data_ptr<float>(), conv_bias.data_ptr<float>(), bias.data_ptr<float>(), scale.data_ptr<float>(), output.data_ptr<float>(), N, C_in, C_out, H, W, H_out, W_out ); // Group norm in-place on output dim3 gn_grid(N, num_groups); dim3 gn_block(256); group_norm_kernel<<<gn_grid, gn_block>>>( output.data_ptr<float>(), output.data_ptr<float>(), gn_weight.data_ptr<float>(), gn_bias.data_ptr<float>(), N, C_out, H_out, W_out, num_groups, 1e-5f ); return output; } ''' cpp_source = ''' torch::Tensor fused_forward( torch::Tensor input, torch::Tensor weight, torch::Tensor conv_bias, torch::Tensor bias, torch::Tensor scale, torch::Tensor gn_weight, torch::Tensor gn_bias, int num_groups ); ''' fused_ops = load_inline( name='fused_ops', cpp_sources=cpp_source, cuda_sources=cuda_source, functions=['fused_forward'], extra_cuda_cflags=['-O3', '--use_fast_math'], verbose=False, ) class ModelNew(nn.Module): def __init__(self, in_channels, out_channels, kernel_size, num_groups, bias_shape, scale_shape): super(ModelNew, self).__init__() self.conv = nn.Conv2d(in_channels, out_channels, kernel_size) self.bias = nn.Parameter(torch.randn(bias_shape)) self.scale = nn.Parameter(torch.randn(scale_shape)) self.group_norm = nn.GroupNorm(num_groups, out_channels) def forward(self, x): return fused_ops.fused_forward( x, self.conv.weight, self.conv.bias, self.bias, self.scale, self.group_norm.weight, self.group_norm.bias, self.group_norm.num_groups ) ``` Wait, I need to be careful. `self.group_norm` will have weight and bias because affine=True is default, but those might be None if affine=False. Let me rewrite the group norm kernel with float4: ```cuda __global__ void group_norm_kernel( const float* __restrict__ in, float* __restrict__ out, const float* __restrict__ gamma, const float* __restrict__ beta, int N, int C, int H, int W, int num_groups, float eps ) { int b = blockIdx.x; offset > 0; offset /= 2) { sum += __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, sum, offset); sum_sq += __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, sum_sq, offset); } __shared__ float sh_sum[8]; __shared__ float sh_sum_sq[8]; int lane = tid % 32; o4.w = (v4.w - mean) * inv_std * g_val + b_val; reinterpret_cast<float4*>(g_out)[idx] = o4; } } ``` This is much more efficient! Here's the plan for the kernel code: ```python import torch import torch.nn as nn from torch.utils.cpp_extension import load_inline CUDA_SOURCE = r''' #include <torch/extension.h> #include <cuda_runtime.h> __global__ void fused_conv_kernel( const float* __restrict__ input, const float* __restrict__ weight, const float* __restrict__ conv_bias, const float* __restrict__ bias, const float* __restrict__ scale, float* __restrict__ output, int N, int C_in, int C_out, int H, int W, int H_out, int W_out ) { int bx = blockIdx.x; int by = blockIdx.y; int bz = blockIdx.z; int tx = threadIdx.x; int ty = threadIdx.y; int out_h = by * 16 + ty; int out_w = bz * 16 + tx; if (out_h >= H_out || out_w >= W_out) return; extern __shared__ float smem[]; float* smem_input = smem; float* smem_weight = smem + (C_in * 18 * 18); int tid = ty * 16 + tx; int total_weights = C_out * C_in * 9; for (int i = tid; i < total_weights; i += 256) { smem_weight[i] = weight[i]; } int tile_h = by * 16; int tile_w = bz * 16; int total_input = C_in * 18 * 18; for (int i = tid; i < total_input; i += 256) { int c_in = i / (18 * 18); int rem = i % (18 * 18); int ih = rem / 18; int iw = rem % 18; int h = tile_h + ih - 1; int w = tile_w + iw - 1; float val = 0.0f; if (h >= 0 && h < H && w >= 0 && w < W) { val = input[((bx * C_in + c_in) * H + h) * W + w]; } smem_input[i] = val; } __syncthreads(); for (int c_out_base = 0; c_out_base < C_out; c_out_base += 8) { float acc[8]; #pragma unroll for (int i = 0; i < 8; i++) { acc[i] = conv_bias[c_out_base + i]; } #pragma unroll for (int c_in = 0; c_in < C_in; c_in++) { #pragma unroll for (int kh = 0; kh < 3; kh++) { #pragma unroll for (int kw = 0; kw < 3; kw++) { float v = smem_input[(c_in * 18 + (ty + kh)) * 18 + (tx + kw)]; #pragma unroll for (int i = 0; i < 8; i++) { acc[i] += v * smem_weight[((c_out_base + i) * C_in + c_in) * 9 + kh * 3 + kw]; } } } } #pragma unroll for (int i = 0; i < 8; i++) { int c = c_out_base + i; float val = (acc[i] + bias[c]) * scale[c]; val = 1.0f / (1.0f + expf(-val)); output[((bx * C_out + c) * H_out + out_h) * W_out + out_w] = val; } } } __global__ void group_norm_kernel( const float* __restrict__ in, float* __restrict__ out, const float* __restrict__ gamma, const float* __restrict__ beta, int N, int C, int H, int W, int num_groups, float eps ) { int b = blockIdx.x; int g = blockIdx.y; int tid = threadIdx.x; int group_size = C / num_groups; int elems_per_channel = H * W; int group_elems = group_size * elems_per_channel; int num_f4 = group_elems / 4; int channel_start = g * group_size; const float* g_in = in + ((long long)b * C + channel_start) * elems_per_channel; float* g_out = out + ((long long)b * C + channel_start) * elems_per_channel; float sum = 0.0f; float sum_sq = 0.0f; for (int idx = tid; idx < num_f4; idx += blockDim.x) { float4 v4 = reinterpret_cast<const float4*>(g_in)[idx]; sum += v4.x + v4.y + v4.z + v4.w; sum_sq += v4.x * v4.x + v4.y * v4.y + v4.z * v4.z + v4.w * v4.w; } #pragma unroll for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset /= 2) { sum += __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, sum, offset); sum_sq += __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, sum_sq, offset); } __shared__ float sh_sum[8]; __shared__ float sh_sum_sq[8]; int lane = tid % 32; int warp_id = tid / 32; int num_warps = blockDim.x / 32; if (lane == 0 && warp_id < num_warps) { sh_sum[warp_id] = sum; sh_sum_sq[warp_id] = sum_sq; } __syncthreads(); if (tid < num_warps) { sum = sh_sum[tid]; sum_sq = sh_sum_sq[tid]; #pragma unroll for (int offset = num_warps / 2; offset > 0; offset /= 2) { sum += __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, sum, offset); sum_sq += __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, sum_sq, offset); } if (tid == 0) { sh_sum[0] = sum; sh_sum_sq[0] = sum_sq; } } __syncthreads(); float mean = sh_sum[0] / group_elems; float var = sh_sum_sq[0] / group_elems - mean * mean; if (var < 0.0f) var = 0.0f; float inv_std = rsqrtf(var + eps); for (int idx = tid; idx < num_f4; idx += blockDim.x) { int global_idx = idx * 4; int c_offset = global_idx / elems_per_channel; int c = channel_start + c_offset; float g_val = gamma[c]; float b_val = beta[c]; float4 v4 = reinterpret_cast<const float4*>(g_in)[idx]; float4 o4; o4.x = (v4.x - mean) * inv_std * g_val + b_val; o4.y = (v4.y - mean) * inv_std * g_val + b_val; o4.z = (v4.z - mean) * inv_std * g_val + b_val; o4.w = (v4.w - mean) * inv_std * g_val + b_val; reinterpret_cast<float4*>(g_out)[idx] = o4; } } torch::Tensor fused_forward( torch::Tensor input, torch::Tensor weight, torch::Tensor conv_bias, torch::Tensor bias, torch::Tensor scale, torch::Tensor gn_weight, torch::Tensor gn_bias, int num_groups ) { int N = input.size(0); int C_in = input.size(1); int H = input.size(2); int W = input.size(3); int C_out = weight.size(0); int H_out = H - 2; int W_out = W - 2; auto output = torch::empty({N, C_out, H_out, W_out}, input.options()); dim3 block(16, 16); dim3 grid(N, (H_out + 15) / 16, (W_out + 15) / 16); size_t smem_size = (C_in * 18 * 18 + C_out * C_in * 9) * sizeof(float); fused_conv_kernel<<<grid, block, smem_size, at::cuda::getCurrentCUDAStream().stream()>>>( input.data_ptr<float>(), weight.data_ptr<float>(), conv_bias.data_ptr<float>(), bias.data_ptr<float>(), scale.data_ptr<float>(), output.data_ptr<float>(), N, C_in, C_out, H, W, H_out, W_out ); dim3 gn_grid(N, num_groups); dim3 gn_block(256); group_norm_kernel<<<gn_grid, gn_block, 0, at::cuda::getCurrentCUDAStream().stream()>>>( output.data_ptr<float>(), output.data_ptr<float>(), gn_weight.data_ptr<float>(), gn_bias.data_ptr<float>(), N, C_out, H_out, W_out, num_groups, 1e-5f ); return output; } ''' ``` Wait, I used `reinterpret_cast<const float4*>(g_in)[idx]`. This assumes `g_in` is aligned to 16 bytes. I verified it is, but let me double check by computing offsets. Actually, in PyTorch, the tensor base pointer is aligned to 256 bytes typically. And `((long long)b * C + channel_start) * elems_per_channel` gives the element offset. The byte offset is `offset * sizeof(float)`. For `b=0, channel_start=0`, offset=0, aligned. For `b=1`, offset = C * elems_per_channel = 32 * 64516 = 2064512. 2064512 * 4 = 8258048. Is this divisible by 16? 8258048 / 16 = 516128. Yes. For `channel_start=4`, offset = 4 * 64516 = 258064. 258064 * 4 = 1032256. 1032256 / 16 = 64516. Yes. So alignment is guaranteed for all blocks. One more thing: `float4` requires 16-byte alignment. The compiler will generate `LDG.E.128` or `LDS.128` instructions. Since the pointer is aligned, these are valid. Conv: sum of products. Input is in [0,1], weights are random ~N(0,1), conv_bias is ~N(0,1). After conv, values might be large (~72 terms, each ~N(0,1) product, so std ~sqrt(72) ≈ 8.5). Adding conv_bias (std 1) is minor. 2. Add bias: random ~N(0,1), negligible effect. 3. Scale: random ~N(0,1). So output might have std ~8.5 * |scale|. Since scale is random, values could be quite large. 4. Sigmoid: clamps everything to [0,1]. For large values, sigmoid approaches 0 or 1. 5. Group norm: normalizes by mean/var over groups. Potential numerical issues: - For very large conv outputs before sigmoid, `expf(-val)` might underflow/overflow. As discussed, the formula handles this correctly. - In group norm, if all values are identical (e.g., if sigmoid saturates to all 1.0s or all 0.0s), variance is 0, and we add `eps=1e-5` to avoid division by zero. Standard. The PyTorch reference uses the same operations. We should match. One more issue: the `conv` kernel computes the convolution directly. PyTorch uses cuDNN which might use different algorithms (e.g., Winograd, implicit GEMM). The numerical results might differ slightly due to different accumulation orders. FP32 sum of 72 products might have tiny differences based on associativity. With tolerance 1e-4, this should be fine. But let me think about accumulation order: - My kernel: for each (c_out, c_in, kh, kw), we accumulate in a specific order: outer loop c_in=0..7, kh=0..2, kw=0..2. Thread 0 convolves the same way as thread 1. - cuDNN might reorder the accumulation or split it across multiple operations. The difference in fp32 for summing 72 numbers could be up to ~71 * eps * magnitude ≈ 71 * 1e-7 * 10 ≈ 7e-5. That's close to 1e-4! One more optimization: I notice that in the conv kernel, the pattern `smem_input[(c_in * 18 + (ty + kh)) * 18 + (tx + kw)]` accesses SMEM with varying `c_in`. Between `c_in` iterations, the address changes by 18*18 = 324 floats = 1296 bytes. With 32 banks and 4-byte floats, 1296 / 4 = 324 banks. 324 % 32 = 4. So consecutive c_in accesses are 4 banks apart. One last thing: in the `fused_conv_kernel`, I have `if (out_h >= H_out || out_w >= W_out) return;` at the beginning. This means boundary threads return early and don't participate in shared memory loading. callcompile_kernel { "kernel_code": "\nimport torch\nimport torch.nn as nn\nfrom torch.utils.cpp_extension import load_inline\n\nCUDA_SOURCE = r'''\n#include <torch/extension.h>\n#include <cuda_runtime.h>\n\n__global__ void fused_conv_kernel(\n const float* __restrict__ input,\n const float* __restrict__ weight,\n const float* __restrict__ conv_bias,\n const float* __restrict__ bias,\n const float* __restrict__ scale,\n float* __restrict__ output,\n int N, int C_in, int C_out, int H, int W, int H_out, int W_out\n) {\n int bx = blockIdx.x;\n int by = blockIdx.y;\n int bz = blockIdx.z;\n int tx = threadIdx.x;\n int ty = threadIdx.y;\n \n int out_h = by * 16 + ty;\n int out_w = bz * 16 + tx;\n bool valid = (out_h < H_out && out_w < W_out);\n \n extern __shared__ float smem[];\n float* smem_input = smem;\n float* smem_weight = smem + (C_in * 18 * 18);\n \n int tid = ty * 16 + tx;\n \n int total_weights = C_out * C_in * 9;\n for (int i = tid; i < total_weights; i += 256) {\n smem_weight[i] = weight[i];\n }\n \n int tile_h = by * 16;\n int tile_w = bz * 16;\n int total_input = C_in * 18 * 18;\n for (int i = tid; i < total_input; i += 256) {\n int c_in = i / (18 * 18);\n int rem = i % (18 * 18);\n int ih = rem / 18;\n int iw = rem % 18;\n int h = tile_h + ih - 1;\n int w = tile_w + iw - 1;\n float val = 0.0f;\n if (h >= 0 && h < H && w >= 0 && w < W) {\n val = input[((bx * C_in + c_in) * H + h) * W + w];\n }\n smem_input[i] = val;\n }\n \n __syncthreads();\n \n if (valid) {\n for (int c_out_base = 0; c_out_base < C_out; c_out_base += 8) {\n float acc[8];\n #pragma unroll\n for (int i = 0; i < 8; i++) {\n acc[i] = conv_bias[c_out_base + i];\n }\n \n #pragma unroll\n for (int c_in = 0; c_in < C_in; c_in++) {\n #pragma unroll\n for (int kh = 0; kh < 3; kh++) {\n #pragma unroll\n for (int kw = 0; kw < 3; kw++) {\n float v = smem_input[(c_in * 18 + (ty + kh)) * 18 + (tx + kw)];\n #pragma unroll\n for (int i = 0; i < 8; i++) {\n acc[i] += v * smem_weight[((c_out_base + i) * C_in + c_in) * 9 + kh * 3 + kw];\n }\n }\n }\n }\n \n #pragma unroll\n for (int i = 0; i < 8; i++) {\n int c = c_out_base + i;\n float val = (acc[i] + bias[c]) * scale[c];\n val = 1.0f / (1.0f + expf(-val));\n output[((bx * C_out + c) * H_out + out_h) * W_out + out_w] = val;\n }\n }\n }\n}\n\n__global__ void group_norm_kernel(\n const float* __restrict__ in,\n float* __restrict__ out,\n const float* __restrict__ gamma,\n const float* __restrict__ beta,\n int N, int C, int H, int W, int num_groups, float eps\n) {\n int b = blockIdx.x;\n int g = blockIdx.y;\n int tid = threadIdx.x;\n int group_size = C / num_groups;\n int elems_per_channel = H * W;\n int group_elems = group_size * elems_per_channel;\n int num_f4 = group_elems / 4;\n int channel_start = g * group_size;\n \n const float* g_in = in + ((long long)b * C + channel_start) * elems_per_channel;\n float* g_out = out + ((long long)b * C + channel_start) * elems_per_channel;\n \n float sum = 0.0f;\n float sum_sq = 0.0f;\n \n for (int idx = tid; idx < num_f4; idx += blockDim.x) {\n float4 v4 = reinterpret_cast<const float4*>(g_in)[idx];\n sum += v4.x + v4.y + v4.z + v4.w;\n sum_sq += v4.x * v4.x + v4.y * v4.y + v4.z * v4.z + v4.w * v4.w;\n }\n \n #pragma unroll\n for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset /= 2) {\n sum += __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, sum, offset);\n sum_sq += __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, sum_sq, offset);\n }\n \n __shared__ float sh_sum[8];\n __shared__ float sh_sum_sq[8];\n int lane = tid % 32;\n int warp_id = tid / 32;\n int num_warps = blockDim.x / 32;\n \n if (lane == 0 && warp_id < num_warps) {\n sh_sum[warp_id] = sum;\n sh_sum_sq[warp_id] = sum_sq;\n }\n __syncthreads();\n \n if (tid < num_warps) {\n sum = sh_sum[tid];\n sum_sq = sh_sum_sq[tid];\n #pragma unroll\n for (int offset = num_warps / 2; offset > 0; offset /= 2) {\n sum += __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, sum, offset);\n sum_sq += __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, sum_sq, offset);\n }\n if (tid == 0) {\n sh_sum[0] = sum;\n sh_sum_sq[0] = sum_sq;\n }\n }\n __syncthreads();\n \n float mean = sh_sum[0] / group_elems;\n float var = sh_sum_sq[0] / group_elems - mean * mean;\n if (var < 0.0f) var = 0.0f;\n float inv_std = rsqrtf(var + eps);\n \n for (int idx = tid; idx < num_f4; idx += blockDim.x) {\n int global_idx = idx * 4;\n int c_offset = global_idx / elems_per_channel;\n int c = channel_start + c_offset;\n float g_val = gamma[c];\n float b_val = beta[c];\n \n float4 v4 = reinterpret_cast<const float4*>(g_in)[idx];\n float4 o4;\n o4.x = (v4.x - mean) * inv_std * g_val + b_val;\n o4.y = (v4.y - mean) * inv_std * g_val + b_val;\n o4.z = (v4.z - mean) * inv_std * g_val + b_val;\n o4.w = (v4.w - mean) * inv_std * g_val + b_val;\n Compute convolution\n acc = tl.zeros([BLOCK_SIZE], dtype=tl.float32)\n for c_in in range(C_in):\n for kh in range(3):\n for kw in range(3):\n in_h = out_h + kh\n in_w = out_w + kw\n in_idx = ((batch_idx * C_in + c_in) * H + in_h) * W + in_w\n w_idx = ((out_channel_idx * C_in + c_in) * 3 + kh) * 3 + kw\n \n in_mask = mask & (in_h >= 0) & (in_h < H) & (in_w >= 0) & (in_w < W)\n val = tl.load(input_ptr + in_idx, mask=in_mask, other=0.0)\n w = tl.load(weight_ptr + w_idx)\n acc += val * w\n \n # Add bias, scale, sigmoid\n b = tl.load(bias_ptr + out_channel_idx)\n s = tl.load(scale_ptr + out_channel_idx)\n \n acc = (acc + b) * s\n acc = tl.sigmoid(acc)\n \n out_idx = ((batch_idx * C_out + out_channel_idx) * H_out + out_h) * W_out + out_w\n tl.store(output_ptr + out_idx, acc, mask=mask)\n \n @triton.jit\n def group_norm_kernel(\n input_ptr, output_ptr, gamma_ptr, beta_ptr,\n N, C, H, W, num_groups, eps,\n BLOCK_SIZE: tl.constexpr\n ):\n batch_idx = tl.program_id(0)\n group_idx = tl.program_id(1)\n \n group_size = C // num_groups\n channels_per_group = group_size\n pixels_per_channel = H * W\n total_pixels = channels_per_group * pixels_per_channel\n \n # Compute mean and variance\n c_start = group_idx * group_size\n \n sum_val = 0.0\n sum_sq = 0.0\n count = 0\n \n for c_offset in range(channels_per_group):\n c = c_start + c_offset\n for pixel_start in range(0, pixels_per_channel, BLOCK_SIZE):\n h = tl.arange(0, BLOCK_SIZE)\n pixel_idx = pixel_start + h\n mask = pixel_idx < pixels_per_channel\n \n ph = pixel_idx // W\n pw = pixel_idx % W\n \n idx = ((batch_idx * C + c) * H + ph) * W + pw\n val = tl.load(input_ptr + idx, mask=mask, other=0.0)\n sum_val += tl.sum(val, axis=0)\n sum_sq += tl.sum(val * val, axis=0)\n count += tl.sum(mask.to(tl.int32), axis=0)\n \n mean = sum_val / total_pixels\n var = sum_sq / total_pixels - mean * mean\n inv_std = tl.rsqrt(var + eps)\n \n # Normalize\n for c_offset in range(channels_per_group):\n c = c_start + c_offset\n g = tl.load(gamma_ptr + c)\n b = tl.load(beta_ptr + c)\n \n for pixel_start in range(0, pixels_per_channel, BLOCK_SIZE):\n h = tl.arange(0, BLOCK_SIZE)\n pixel_idx = pixel_start + h\n mask = pixel_idx < pixels_per_channel\n \n ph = pixel_idx // W\n pw = pixel_idx % W\n \n idx = ((batch_idx * C + c) * H + ph) * W + pw\n val = tl.load(input_ptr + idx, mask=mask, other=0.0)\n normalized = (val - mean) * inv_std * g + b\n tl.store(output_ptr + idx, normalized, mask=mask)\n \n def fused_forward_triton(input, weight, conv_bias, bias, scale, gn_weight, gn_bias, num_groups):\n N, C_in, H, W = input.shape\n C_out = weight.shape[0]\n H_out, W_out = H - 2, W - 2\n \n output = torch.empty(N, C_out, H_out, W_out, device=input.device, dtype=input.dtype)\n \n grid = (N, C_out)\n BLOCK_SIZE = 256\n \n fused_conv_bias_scale_sigmoid_kernel[grid](\n input, weight, bias, scale, output,\n N, C_in, C_out, H, W, H_out, W_out,\n BLOCK_SIZE=BLOCK_SIZE\n )\n \n # Group norm\n gn_grid = (N, num_groups)\n group_norm_kernel[gn_grid](\n output, output, gn_weight, gn_bias,\n N, C_out, H_out, W_out, num_groups, 1e-5,\n BLOCK_SIZE=BLOCK_SIZE\n )\n \n return output\n \n class ModelNew(nn.Module):\n def __init__(self, in_channels, out_channels, kernel_size, num_groups, bias_shape, scale_shape):\n super(ModelNew, self).__init__()\n self.conv = nn.Conv2d(in_channels, out_channels, kernel_size)\n self.bias = nn.Parameter(torch.randn(bias_shape))\n self.scale = nn.Parameter(torch.randn(scale_shape))\n self.group_norm = nn.GroupNorm(num_groups, out_channels)\n\n def forward(self, x):\n return fused_forward_triton(\n x,\n self.conv.weight,\n self.conv.bias,\n self.bias,\n self.scale,\n self.group_norm.weight,\n self.group_norm.bias,\n self.group_norm.num_groups\n )\nelse:\n # Let me finalize the kernel code: ```python import torch import torch.nn as nn from torch.utils.cpp_extension import load_inline CUDA_SOURCE = r''' #include <torch/extension.h> __global__ void fused_conv_sigmoid_gn_kernel( const float* __restrict__ input, const float* __restrict__ weight, const float* __restrict__ conv_bias, const float* __restrict__ bias, const float* __restrict__ scale, float* __restrict__ output, const float* __restrict__ gn_weight, const float* __restrict__ gn_beta, int N, int C_in, int C_out, int H, int W, int H_out, int W_out, int num_groups, float eps ) { int n = blockIdx.x; int h = blockIdx.y * blockDim.y + threadIdx.y; int w = blockIdx.z * blockDim.z + threadIdx.z; if (h >= H_out || w >= W_out) return; int group_size = C_out / num_groups; int tid = threadIdx.x; // Process channels in chunks of num_threads or similar for (int c_base = 0; c_base < C_out; c_base += blockDim.x) { int c = c_base + tid; if (c < C_out) { float acc = conv_bias[c]; for (int ci = 0; ci < C_in; ci++) { for (int kh = 0; kh < 3; kh++) { for (int kw = 0; kw < 3; kw++) { float v = input[((n * C_in + ci) * H + (h + kh)) * W + (w + kw)]; float wt = weight[((c * C_in + ci) * 3 + kh) * 3 + kw]; acc += v * wt; } } } acc = (acc + bias[c]) * scale[c]; acc = 1.0f / (1.0f + expf(-acc)); output[((n * C_out + c) * H_out + h) * W_out + w] = acc; } } __syncthreads(); // Group norm int g = tid / group_size; if (g >= num_groups) return; // Compute mean and var for this group int c_start = g * group_size; float sum = 0.0f, sum_sq = 0.0f; int elems = group_size * H_out * W_out; for (int i = tid % group_size; i < elems; i += blockDim.x) { int c_off = i / (H_out * W_out); int rem = i % (H_out * W_out); int ph = rem / W_out; int pw = rem % W_out; float v = output[((n * C_out + c_start + c_off) * H_out + ph) * W_out + pw]; sum += v; sum_sq += v * v; } // Reduction across threads... this is getting complex } '''; ``` Actually, writing a correct and fast group norm is really tricky. Each output value requires 8*9 = 72 FMAs. Total FMAs = 264M * 72 = 19B FMAs. At 67 TFLOPS = 67,000 GFLOPS, that's 19,000 / 67,000 = 0.28 ms. This is compute bound. So the kernel should be latency-optimized. Using 1 thread per pixel computing all output channels sequentially is actually not bad because: 1. Each thread has good arithmetic intensity 2. Weight accesses are uniform across threads and can be cached in L1 3. Input accesses have spatial locality But with 16x16 threads per block and each thread doing 2304 FMAs, that's a lot of registers. This is on the high side but acceptable for H100. - 4 channel groups per block - Each thread computes 1 pixel and 8 output channels (since 32 channels / 4 groups = 8 channels per group) Actually, let me do: - 1 block per output pixel (h, w) - 1 thread per output channel group of 4 - Out_channels = 32, so 8 threads per block - Each thread computes 4 output channels for 1 pixel - Total blocks = N * H_out * W_out = 128 * 254 * 254 = 8,258,048 blocks - This is way too many blocks for the GPU to handle efficiently Better: 1 block per spatial tile of 16x16 pixels. - Threads = (4, 8, 8) = 256 threads - Each thread computes 8 output channels for 4 pixels? No, that's confusing.
